864 <section id="_1">

865

866 <h2>

867General Information About Osteosarcoma and Undifferentiated Pleomorphic Sarcoma (UPS) (Formerly Called Malignant Fibrous Histiocytoma [MFH]) of Bone</h2>

868

869 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

870<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

871<h6>

872In This Section</h6>

873<ul>

874<li>

875<a href="#_1802_toc">

876Disease Overview</a>

877</li>

878<li>

879<a href="#_1822_toc">

880Diagnostic Evaluation</a>

881</li>

882<li>

883<a href="#_363_toc">

884Prognostic Factors<ul>

885<li>

886<a href="#_365_toc">

887Primary tumor site</a>

888</li>

889<li>

890<a href="#_368_toc">

891Size of the primary tumor</a>

892</li>

893<li>

894<a href="#_370_toc">

895Presence of clinically detectable metastatic disease</a>

896</li>

897<li>

898<a href="#_372_toc">

899Surgical resectability of the primary tumor</a>

900</li>

901<li>

902<a href="#_374_toc">

903Degree of tumor necrosis after neoadjuvant chemotherapy</a>

904</li>

905<li>

906<a href="#_1975_toc">

907Age and sex</a>

908</li>

909<li>

911Other possible prognostic factors</a>

912</li>

913</ul>

914</a>

915</li>

916</ul>

917</nav>

918<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

919<a href="/types/bone/patient/osteosarcoma-treatment-pdq">

920Go to Patient Version</a>

921</div>

922<section id="_1802">

923<h3 id="_1802_toc">

924Disease Overview</h3>

925<p id="_4" tabindex="-1">

926Osteosarcoma occurs predominantly in adolescents and young

927adults. Review of data from the National Cancer Institute's National Childhood Cancer Registry resulted in an estimated osteosarcoma incidence rate of 5.4 cases per 1 million each year in people aged 0 to 19 years and 4 cases per 1 million each year in people younger than 40 years.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9281</a>

929] The U.S. Census Bureau estimated that there were 82 million people between the ages of 0 and 19 years, resulting in an incidence of roughly 440 cases per year in this age group. </p>

930<p id="_1803" tabindex="-1">

931Osteosarcoma accounts for approximately 5% of childhood tumors. In

932children and adolescents, more than 50% of these tumors arise from the long bones around the

933knee. Osteosarcoma is rarely observed in soft tissue or visceral organs. There appears to be no difference in presenting symptoms, tumor location, and outcome for younger patients (&lt;12 years) compared with adolescents.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9342</a>

935,<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9363</a>

937] </p>

938<p id="_1804" tabindex="-1">

939Two trials conducted in the 1980s were designed to determine whether chemotherapy altered the natural history of osteosarcoma after surgical removal of the primary tumor. The outcome of these patients recapitulated the historical

940experience before 1970; more than one-half of these patients developed metastases

941within 6 months of diagnosis, and overall, approximately 90% developed recurrent

942disease within 2 years of diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

9434</a>

944] Overall survival (OS) for patients

945treated with surgery alone was statistically inferior.[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

9465</a>

947] The natural history

948of osteosarcoma has not changed over time, and fewer than 20% of patients with

949localized, resectable primary tumors treated with surgery alone can be expected

950to survive free of relapse.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

9514</a>

952,<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

9536</a>

954]; [<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

9557</a>

956][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

957Level of evidence A1</a>

958]</p>

959<p id="_1926" tabindex="-1">

960In 2013, the World Health Organization (WHO) published an update to the Classification of Tumors of Soft Tissue and Bone.[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

9618</a>

962] They removed the term <em>

963malignant fibrous histiocytoma (MFH)</em>

964 and replaced it with <em>

965undifferentiated pleomorphic sarcoma (UPS)</em>

966. This type of sarcoma is much more common in soft tissues; however, it does arise in bone. In bone, it has features that are histologically similar to osteosarcoma, but it does not produce osteoid. Most of the literature describing the clinical behavior and response to therapy for this histology in bone was published before the 2013 WHO update, and a search for <em>

967UPS of bone</em>

968 will not retrieve these articles. The citations in this summary appear with their titles as published; therefore, many references will describe <em>

969MFH of bone</em>

970, a condition now called <em>

971UPS of bone</em>

972.</p>

973</section>

974<section id="_1822">

975<h3 id="_1822_toc">

976Diagnostic Evaluation</h3>

977<p id="_247" tabindex="-1">

978Osteosarcoma can be diagnosed by core needle biopsy or open surgical biopsy. It is preferable that the biopsy be performed by a surgeon skilled in the techniques of limb sparing (removal of the malignant bone tumor without amputation and replacement of bones or joints with allografts or prosthetic devices). In these cases, the original biopsy incision placement is crucial. Inappropriate alignment of the biopsy or inadvertent contamination of soft tissues can render subsequent limb-preserving reconstructive surgery impossible.</p>

979</section>

980<section id="_363">

981<h3 id="_363_toc">

982Prognostic Factors</h3>

983<p id="_87" tabindex="-1">

984Dramatic improvements in survival have been achieved for children and adolescents with cancer. Between 1975 and 2020, childhood cancer mortality decreased by more than 50%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9851</a>

986,<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

9879</a>

988,<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

98910</a>

990] For osteosarcoma, the 5-year relative survival rate increased over the same time from 40% to 72% in children younger than 15 years and from 56% to approximately 71% in adolescents aged 15 to 19 years. However, there has been no substantial improvement since the 1980s.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9911</a>

992,<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

99311</a>

994]</p>

995<p id="_448" tabindex="-1">

996In general, prognostic factors for osteosarcoma have not been helpful in identifying patients who might benefit from treatment intensification or who might require less therapy while maintaining an excellent outcome.</p>

997<p id="_364" tabindex="-1">

998Factors that may influence outcome include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

99912</a>

1000] </p>

1001<div class="pdq-content-list">

1002<ul id="_445">

1003<li>

1004<a href="/types/bone/hp/osteosarcoma-treatment-pdq#_365">

1005Primary tumor site</a>

1006.</li>

1007<li>

1008<a href="/types/bone/hp/osteosarcoma-treatment-pdq#_368">

1009Size of the primary tumor</a>

1010.</li>

1011<li>

1012<a href="/types/bone/hp/osteosarcoma-treatment-pdq#_370">

1013Presence of clinically detectable metastatic disease</a>

1014. </li>

1015<li>

1016<a href="/types/bone/hp/osteosarcoma-treatment-pdq#_372">

1017Surgical resectability of primary tumor</a>

1018.</li>

1019<li>

1020<a href="/types/bone/hp/osteosarcoma-treatment-pdq#_374">

1021Degree of tumor necrosis after administration of neoadjuvant chemotherapy</a>

1022. </li>

1023<li>

1024<a href="/types/bone/hp/osteosarcoma-treatment-pdq#_1975">

1025Age and sex</a>

1026.</li>

1027<li>

1028<a href="/types/bone/hp/osteosarcoma-treatment-pdq#_376">

1029Other possible prognostic factors</a>

1030.</li>

1031</ul>

1032</div>

1033<section id="_365">

1034<h4 id="_365_toc">

1035Primary tumor site</h4>

1036<p id="_344" tabindex="-1">

1037The site of the primary tumor is a significant prognostic factor for patients with localized disease. Among extremity tumors, distal sites have a more favorable prognosis than do proximal sites. Axial skeleton primary tumors are associated with the greatest risk of progression and death, primarily related to the inability to achieve a complete surgical resection.</p>

1038<p id="_1805" tabindex="-1">

1039Prognostic considerations for the axial skeleton and extraskeletal sites are as follows:</p>

1040<div class="pdq-content-list">

1041<ul id="_345">

1042<li>

1043<strong>

1044Pelvis:</strong>

1045 Pelvic osteosarcomas make up 7% to 9% of all osteosarcomas; survival rates for patients with pelvic primary tumors are 20% to 47%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

104613</a>

1047-<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

104815</a>

1049] Complete surgical resection is associated with a positive outcome for osteosarcoma of the pelvis in some cohorts of patients.[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

105013</a>

1051,<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

105216</a>

1053]</li>

1054<li>

1055<strong>

1056Craniofacial/head and neck:</strong>

1057 In patients with craniofacial osteosarcoma, those with primary sites in the mandible and maxilla have a better prognosis than do patients with other primary sites in the head and neck.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

105817</a>

1059-<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

106019</a>

1061] For patients with osteosarcoma of craniofacial bones, complete resection of the primary tumor with negative margins is essential for cure.[<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

106220</a>

1063-<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

106422</a>

1065] When treated with surgery alone, patients who have osteosarcoma of the head and neck have a better prognosis than those who have appendicular lesions. <p id="_346" tabindex="-1">

1066Despite a relatively high rate of inferior necrosis after neoadjuvant chemotherapy, fewer patients with craniofacial primaries develop systemic metastases than do patients with osteosarcoma originating in the extremities.[<a href="#cit/section_1.23">

106723</a>

1068-<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

106925</a>

1070] </p>

1071 <p id="_347" tabindex="-1">

1072A meta-analysis concluded that systemic adjuvant chemotherapy improves the prognosis for patients with osteosarcoma of the head and neck, while small series have not shown such a benefit.[<a href="#cit/section_1.23">

107323</a>

1074-<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

107525</a>

1076] Another large meta-analysis detected no benefit of chemotherapy for patients with osteosarcoma of the head and neck, but suggested that incorporating chemotherapy into the treatment plan for patients with high-grade tumors may improve survival.[<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

107722</a>

1078] A retrospective analysis identified a trend toward better survival in patients with high-grade osteosarcoma of the mandible and maxilla who received adjuvant chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

107922</a>

1080,<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

108126</a>

1082] </p>

1083<p id="_348" tabindex="-1">

1084Radiation therapy was found to improve local control, disease-specific survival, and OS in a retrospective study of patients with osteosarcoma of the craniofacial bones who had positive or uncertain margins after surgical resection.[<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

108527</a>

1086][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1087Level of evidence C1</a>

1088] Radiation-associated craniofacial osteosarcomas are generally high-grade lesions, usually fibroblastic, and tend to recur locally with a high rate of metastasis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.28">

108928</a>

1090]</p>

1091</li>

1092<li>

1093<strong>

1094Extraskeletal:</strong>

1095 Osteosarcoma in extraskeletal sites is rare in children and young adults. With current combined-modality therapy, the outcome of patients with extraskeletal osteosarcoma appears to be similar to that of patients with primary tumors of bone.[<a href="#cit/section_1.29">

109629</a>

1097]</li>

1098</ul>

1099</div>

1100</section>

1101<section id="_368">

1102<h4 id="_368_toc">

1103Size of the primary tumor</h4>

1104<p id="_369" tabindex="-1">

1105In some series, patients with larger tumors appeared to have a worse prognosis than patients with smaller tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

110612</a>

1107,<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

110830</a>

1109,<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

111031</a>

1111] Tumor size has been assessed by longest single dimension, cross-sectional area, or estimate of tumor volume; all assessments have correlated with outcome.</p>

1112<p id="_1806" tabindex="-1">

1113Serum lactate dehydrogenase (LDH), which also correlates with outcome, is a likely surrogate for tumor volume.[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

111414</a>

1115]</p>

1116</section>

1117<section id="_370">

1118<h4 id="_370_toc">

1119Presence of clinically detectable metastatic disease</h4>

1120<p id="_350" tabindex="-1">

1121Patients with localized disease have a much better prognosis than patients with overt metastatic disease. As many as 20% of patients have radiographically detectable metastases at diagnosis, with the lung being the most common site.[<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

112232</a>

1123] The prognosis for patients with metastatic disease appears to be determined largely by site(s) of metastases, number of metastases, and surgical resectability of the metastatic disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

112433</a>

1125,<a href="#cit/section_1.34">

112634</a>

1127]</p>

1128<div class="pdq-content-list">

1129<ul id="_351">

1130<li>

1131<strong>

1132Site of metastases:</strong>

1133 Prognosis appears more favorable for patients with fewer pulmonary nodules and for those with unilateral rather than bilateral pulmonary metastases.[<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

113433</a>

1135] Not all patients with suspected pulmonary metastases at diagnosis have osteosarcoma confirmed at the time of lung resection. In one large series, approximately 25% of patients had exclusively benign lesions removed at the time of surgery.[<a href="#cit/section_1.34">

113634</a>

1137] </li>

1138<li>

1139<strong>

1140Number of metastases:</strong>

1141 Patients with <em>

1142skip</em>

1143 metastases (at least two discontinuous lesions in the same bone) have been reported to have inferior prognoses.[<a href="#cit/section_1.35">

114435</a>

1145] However, an analysis of the German Cooperative Osteosarcoma Study suggests that skip lesions in the same bone do not confer an inferior prognosis if they are included in planned surgical resection. Skip metastasis in a bone other than the primary bone should be considered systemic metastasis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

114636</a>

1147] <p id="_2400" tabindex="-1">

1148Historically, metastasis across a joint was referred to as a skip lesion. Skip lesions across a joint might be considered hematogenous spread and have a worse prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

114936</a>

1150]</p>

1151<p id="_352" tabindex="-1">

1152Patients with multifocal osteosarcoma (defined as multiple bone lesions without a clear primary tumor) have an extremely poor prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.37">

115337</a>

1154,<a href="#cit/section_1.38">

115538</a>

1156]</p>

1157</li>

1158<li>

1159<strong>

1160Surgical resectability of metastases:</strong>

1161 Patients who have complete surgical ablation of the primary and metastatic tumor (when confined to the lung) after chemotherapy may attain long-term survival, although overall event-free survival (EFS) rates remain about 20% to 30% for patients with metastatic disease at diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

116233</a>

1163,<a href="#cit/section_1.34">

116434</a>

1165,<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

116639</a>

1167,<a href="#cit/section_1.40">

116840</a>

1169] </li>

1170</ul>

1171</div>

1172</section>

1173<section id="_372">

1174<h4 id="_372_toc">

1175Surgical resectability of the primary tumor</h4>

1176<p id="_373" tabindex="-1">

1177Resectability of the tumor is a critical prognostic feature because osteosarcoma is relatively resistant to radiation therapy. Complete resection of the primary tumor and any skip lesions with adequate margins is generally considered essential for cure. A retrospective review of patients with craniofacial osteosarcoma performed by the cooperative German-Austrian-Swiss osteosarcoma study group reported that incomplete surgical resection was associated with inferior survival probability.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

117817</a>

1179][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1180Level of evidence C1</a>

1181] In a European cooperative study, the size of the margin was not significant. However, prognosis was better when both the biopsy and resection were performed at a center with orthopedic oncology experience.[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

118214</a>

1183]</p>

1184<p id="_449" tabindex="-1">

1185For patients with axial skeletal primaries who either do not undergo surgery for their primary tumor or who undergo surgery that results in positive margins, radiation therapy may improve survival.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

118616</a>

1187,<a href="#cit/section_1.41">

118841</a>

1189]</p>

1190</section>

1191<section id="_374">

1192<h4 id="_374_toc">

1193Degree of tumor necrosis after neoadjuvant chemotherapy</h4>

1194<p id="_375" tabindex="-1">

1195Most treatment protocols for osteosarcoma use an initial period of systemic chemotherapy before definitive resection of the primary tumor (or resection of sites of metastases). The pathologist assesses necrosis in the resected tumor. Patients with at least 90% necrosis in the primary tumor after induction chemotherapy have a better prognosis than do patients with less necrosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

119630</a>

1197] Patients with less necrosis (&lt;90%) in the primary tumor after initial chemotherapy have a higher rate of recurrence within the first 2 years than do patients with a more favorable amount of necrosis (&#8805;90%).[<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

119842</a>

1199]</p>

1200<p id="_1808" tabindex="-1">

1201Less necrosis should not be interpreted to mean that chemotherapy has been ineffective; cure rates for patients with little or no necrosis after induction chemotherapy are much higher than cure rates for patients who receive no chemotherapy. The EFS rate for patients who receive no adjuvant chemotherapy is approximately 11%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.43">

120243</a>

1203] Many large published series of patients treated with chemotherapy have reported EFS rates of 40% to 50% for patients with little or no necrosis in the primary tumor after initial systemic chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

120444</a>

1205-<a href="#cit/section_1.46">

120646</a>

1207] A review of two consecutive prospective trials performed by the Children&#8217;s Oncology Group showed that histologic necrosis in the primary tumor after initial chemotherapy was affected by the duration and intensity of the initial period of chemotherapy. More necrosis was associated with better outcome in both trials, but the magnitude of the difference between patients with more and less necrosis was diminished with a longer and more intensive period of initial chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.47">

120847</a>

1209][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1210Level of evidence B1</a>

1211]</p>

1212</section>

1213<section id="_1975">

1214<h4 id="_1975_toc">

1215Age and sex</h4>

1216<p id="_1925" tabindex="-1">

1217Patients in the older adolescent and young adult age group, typically defined as age 18 to 40 years, tend to have a worse prognosis. In addition, male sex has been associated with a worse prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

121831</a>

1219,<a href="#cit/section_1.48">

122048</a>

1221,<a href="#cit/section_1.49">

122249</a>

1223] Compared with the other prognostic factors listed, both age and sex have a relatively minor impact on outcome.</p>

1224</section>

1225<section id="_376">

1226<h4 id="_376_toc">

1227Other possible prognostic factors</h4>

1228<p id="_450" tabindex="-1">

1229Other factors that may be prognostic but with either limited or conflicting data include the following:</p>

1230<div class="pdq-content-list">

1231<ul id="_451">

1232<li>

1233<strong>

1234Subsequent neoplasms.</strong>

1235 Patients with osteosarcoma as a subsequent neoplasm, including tumors arising in a radiation field, share the same prognosis as patients with de novo osteosarcoma if they are treated aggressively with complete surgical resection and multiagent chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.50">

123650</a>

1237-<a href="#cit/section_1.53">

123853</a>

1239]<p id="_349" tabindex="-1">

1240 In a German series, approximately 25% of patients with craniofacial osteosarcoma had osteosarcoma as a second tumor, and in 8 of these 13 patients, osteosarcoma arose after treatment for retinoblastoma. In this series, there was no difference in outcome for primary or secondary craniofacial osteosarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

124117</a>

1242]</p>

1243</li>

1244<li>

1245<strong>

1246Laboratory abnormalities.</strong>

1247 Possible prognostic factors identified for patients with conventional localized high-grade osteosarcoma include LDH level, alkaline phosphatase level, and histologic subtype.[<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

124830</a>

1249,<a href="#cit/section_1.45">

125045</a>

1251,<a href="#cit/section_1.48">

125248</a>

1253,<a href="#cit/section_1.54">

125454</a>

1255-<a href="#cit/section_1.57">

125657</a>

1257]</li>

1258<li>

1259<strong>

1260Body mass index. </strong>

1261Higher body mass index at initial presentation is associated with worse OS.[<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

126258</a>

1263]</li>

1264<li>

1265<strong>

1266Pathological fracture. </strong>

1267Some studies have suggested that a pathological fracture at diagnosis or during preoperative chemotherapy does not have adverse prognostic significance.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

12686</a>

1269]; [<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

127059</a>

1271,<a href="#cit/section_1.60">

127260</a>

1273][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1274Level of evidence C1</a>

1275]; [<a href="#cit/section_1.61">

127661</a>

1277][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1278Level of evidence C2</a>

1279]<p id="_1919" tabindex="-1">

1280 However, a systematic review of nine cohort studies examined the impact of pathological fractures on outcome in patients with osteosarcoma. The review included 2,187 patients, 311 of whom had a pathological fracture. The presence of a pathological fracture correlated with decreased EFS and OS.[<a href="#cit/section_1.62">

128162</a>

1282] In two additional series, a pathological fracture at diagnosis was associated with a worse overall outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_1.63">

128363</a>

1284]; [<a href="#cit/section_1.64">

128564</a>

1286][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1287Level of evidence C1</a>

1288] A retrospective analysis of 2,847 patients with osteosarcoma from the German cooperative group identified 321 patients (11.3%) with a pathological fracture before the initiation of systemic therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.65">

128965</a>

1290][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1291Level of evidence C1</a>

1292] In pediatric patients, OS and EFS did not differ significantly between patients with and without a pathological fracture. In adults, the 5-year OS rate in patients with a pathological fracture was 46% versus 69% for patients without a pathological fracture (<em>

1293P</em>

1294 &lt; .001). The 5-year EFS rate in adults was 36% for patients with a pathological fracture versus 56% for patients without a pathological fracture (<em>

1295P</em>

1296 &lt; .001). In a multivariable analysis, the presence of a pathological fracture was not a statistically significant factor for OS or EFS in the total cohort or in pediatric patients. In adult patients, presence of a pathological fracture remained an independent prognostic factor for OS (hazard ratio, 1.893; <em>

1297P</em>

1298 = .013).</p>

1299</li>

1300<li>

1301<strong>

1302Time to definitive surgery.</strong>

1303 In a large series, a delay of 21 days or longer from the time of definitive surgery to the resumption of chemotherapy was an adverse prognostic factor.[<a href="#cit/section_1.66">

130466</a>

1305] </li>

1306<li>

1307<strong>

1308Genetic factors.</strong>

1309 <div class="pdq-content-list">

1310<ul id="_1947" class="list-dash">

1311<li>

1312<em class="gene-name">

1313ERBB2</em>

1314 expression. There are conflicting data concerning the prognostic significance of this human epidermal growth factor.[<a href="#cit/section_1.67">

131567</a>

1316-<a href="#cit/section_1.69">

131769</a>

1318]</li>

1319<li>

1320Tumor cell ploidy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.70">

132170</a>

1322]</li>

1323<li>

1324Specific chromosomal gains or losses.[<a href="#cit/section_1.71">

132571</a>

1326]</li>

1327<li>

1328Loss of heterozygosity of the <em class="gene-name">

1329RB1</em>

1330 gene.[<a href="#cit/section_1.72">

133172</a>

1332,<a href="#cit/section_1.73">

133373</a>

1334]</li>

1335<li>

1336Loss of heterozygosity of the <em class="gene-name">

1337p53</em>

1338 locus.[<a href="#cit/section_1.74">

133974</a>

1340]</li>

1341<li>

1342Increased expression of p-glycoprotein.[<a href="#cit/section_1.75">

134375</a>

1344,<a href="#cit/section_1.76">

134576</a>

1346] A prospective analysis of p-glycoprotein expression determined by immunohistochemistry failed to identify prognostic significance for patients with newly diagnosed osteosarcoma, although earlier studies suggested that overexpression of p-glycoprotein predicted poor outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_1.77">

134777</a>

1348]</li>

1349</ul>

1350</div>

1351<p id="_2401" tabindex="-1">

1352For more information, see the <a href="/types/bone/hp/osteosarcoma-treatment-pdq#_1974">

1353Genomics of Osteosarcoma</a>

1354 section.</p>

1355</li>

1356</ul>

1357</div>

1676 <section id="_1974">

1677

1678 <h2>

1679Genomics of Osteosarcoma</h2>

1680

1681 <div id="_section_1974" class="pdq-sections">

1682<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

1683<h6>

1684In This Section</h6>

1685<ul>

1686<li>

1687<a href="#_sm_CDR0000777834_1_toc">

1688Molecular Features of Osteosarcoma<ul>

1689<li>

1690<a href="#_sm_CDR0000777834_1922_toc">

1691Genetic predisposition to osteosarcoma</a>

1692</li>

1693</ul>

1694</a>

1695</li>

1696</ul>

1697</nav>

1698<section id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1">

1699<h3 id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1_toc">

1700Molecular Features of Osteosarcoma</h3>

1701<p id="_sm_CDR0000777834_3" tabindex="-1">

1702The genomic landscape of osteosarcoma is distinct from that of other childhood cancers. Compared with many adult cancers, it is characterized by an exceptionally high number of structural variants with a relatively small number of single nucleotide variants.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

17031</a>

1704,<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

17052</a>

1706]</p>

1707<p id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1911" tabindex="-1">

1708Key observations regarding the genomic landscape of osteosarcoma are summarized below:</p>

1709<div class="pdq-content-list">

1710<ul id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1912">

1711<li>

1712The number of structural variants observed for osteosarcoma is high, at more than 200 structural variants per genome;[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

17131</a>

1714,<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

17152</a>

1716] thus, osteosarcoma has the most chaotic genome among childhood cancers. The Circos plots shown in Figure 1 illustrate the exceptionally high number of intra- and inter-chromosomal translocations that typify osteosarcoma genomes.<p id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1918" tabindex="-1">

1717<figure id="figure_sm_CDR0000777834_1917" class="image-center">

1718<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/777891.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1719Enlarge</a>

1720<img id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1917" alt="Diagrams of osteosarcoma cases from the NCI TARGET project." title="Diagrams of osteosarcoma cases from the NCI TARGET project." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/777891-750.jpg">

1721<figcaption class="caption-container">

1722Figure 1. Circos plots of osteosarcoma cases from the National Cancer Institute's Therapeutically Applicable Research to Generate Effective Treatments (TARGET) project. The red lines in the interior circle connect chromosome regions involved in either intra- or inter-chromosomal translocations. Osteosarcoma is distinctive from other childhood cancers because it has a large number of intra- and inter-chromosomal translocations. Credit: National Cancer Institute.</figcaption>

1723</figure>

1724</p>

1725</li>

1726<li>

1727The number of mutations per osteosarcoma genome that affect protein sequence (approximately 25 per genome) is higher than that of some other childhood cancers (e.g., Ewing sarcoma and rhabdoid tumors) but is far below that for adult cancers such as melanoma and non-small cell lung cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

17281</a>

1729,<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

17302</a>

1731]</li>

1732<li>

1733Genomic alterations in <em class="gene-name">

1734TP53</em>

1735 are present in most osteosarcoma cases. A distinctive form of <em class="gene-name">

1736TP53</em>

1737 inactivation occurs through structural variations in the first intron of <em class="gene-name">

1738TP53</em>

1739 that lead to disruption of the <em class="gene-name">

1740TP53</em>

1741 gene.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

17421</a>

1743] Other mechanisms of <em class="gene-name">

1744TP53</em>

1745 inactivation are also observed, including missense and nonsense mutations and deletions of the <em class="gene-name">

1746TP53</em>

1747 gene.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

17481</a>

1749,<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

17502</a>

1751] The combination of these various mechanisms for loss of <em class="gene-name">

1752TP53</em>

1753 function leads to biallelic inactivation in most cases of osteosarcoma.</li>

1754<li>

1755<em class="gene-name">

1756MDM2</em>

1757 amplification is observed in a minority of osteosarcoma cases (approximately 5%) and provides another mechanism for loss of <em class="gene-name">

1758TP53</em>

1759 function.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

17601</a>

1761,<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

17622</a>

1763]</li>

1764<li>

1765<em class="gene-name">

1766RB1</em>

1767 is commonly inactivated in osteosarcoma, sometimes by mutation but more commonly by deletion.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

17681</a>

1769,<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

17702</a>

1771]</li>

1772<li>

1773Other genes with recurrent alterations in osteosarcoma include <em class="gene-name">

1774ATRX</em>

1775 and <em class="gene-name">

1776DLG2</em>

1777.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

17781</a>

1779] Additionally, pathway analysis showed that the PI3K/mammalian target of rapamycin (mTOR) pathway was altered by mutation/loss/amplification in approximately one-fourth of patients, with <em class="gene-name">

1780PTEN</em>

1781 mutation/loss being the most common alteration.[<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

17822</a>

1783]</li>

1784<li>

1785A retrospective review of 71 osteosarcoma tumors from 66 patients identified theoretically targetable alterations in 14 patients (21%), including amplification of <em class="gene-name">

1786CDK4</em>

1787 (n = 9) and/or <em class="gene-name">

1788MDM2</em>

1789 (n = 9) and somatic truncating mutations/deletions in <em class="gene-name">

1790BRCA2</em>

1791 (n = 3) and <em class="gene-name">

1792PTCH1</em>

1793 (n = 1). The authors reported mutually exclusive patterns of alterations, suggesting distinct biological subsets defined by gains at 4q12 and 6p12-21. Specifically, potentially targetable gene amplifications at 4q12 involving <em class="gene-name">

1794KIT</em>

1795, <em class="gene-name">

1796KDR</em>

1797, and <em class="gene-name">

1798PDGFRA</em>

1799 were identified in 13 of 66 patients (20%), which showed strong <em class="gene-name">

1800PDGFRA</em>

1801 expression by immunohistochemistry. In another largely nonoverlapping subset of 14 patients (24%) with gains at 6p12-21, <em class="gene-name">

1802VEGFRA</em>

1803 amplification was identified.[<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

18043</a>

1805]</li>

1806<li>

1807The range of mutations reported for osteosarcoma tumors at diagnosis do not provide obvious therapeutic targets, as they primarily reflect loss of tumor suppressor genes (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

1808TP53</em>

1809, <em class="gene-name">

1810RB1</em>

1811, <em class="gene-name">

1812PTEN</em>

1813) rather than activation of targetable oncogenes.</li>

1814</ul>

1815</div>

1816<section id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1922">

1817<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1922_toc">

1818Genetic predisposition to osteosarcoma</h4>

1819<p id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1913" tabindex="-1">

1820Germline mutations in several genes are associated with susceptibility to osteosarcoma. Table 1 summarizes the syndromes and associated genes for these conditions. A recent multi-institutional genomic study of more than 1,200 patients with osteosarcoma revealed pathogenic or likely pathogenic germline variants in autosomal dominant cancer-susceptibility genes in 18% of patients. The frequency of these cancer-susceptibility genes was higher in children aged 10 years or younger.[<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

18214</a>

1822]</p>

1823<section id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1923">

1824<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1923_toc">

1825<em class="gene-name">

1826TP53</em>

1827 mutations</h5>

1828<p id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1919" tabindex="-1">

1829Mutations in <em class="gene-name">

1830TP53</em>

1831 are the most common germline alterations associated with osteosarcoma. Mutations in this gene are found in approximately 70% of patients with Li-Fraumeni syndrome (LFS), which is associated with increased risk of osteosarcoma, breast cancer, various brain cancers, soft tissue sarcomas, and other cancers. While rhabdomyosarcoma is the most common sarcoma arising in patients aged 5 years and younger with <em class="gene-name">

1832TP53</em>

1833-associated LFS, osteosarcoma is the most common sarcoma in children and adolescents aged 6 to 19 years.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

18345</a>

1835] One study observed a high frequency of young osteosarcoma cases (age &lt;30 years) carrying a known LFS-associated or likely LFS-associated <em class="gene-name">

1836TP53</em>

1837 mutation (3.8%) or rare exonic <em class="gene-name">

1838TP53</em>

1839 variant (5.7%), with an overall <em class="gene-name">

1840TP53</em>

1841 mutation frequency of 9.5%.[<a href="#cit/section_2.6">

18426</a>

1843] Other groups have reported lower rates (3%&#8211;7%) of <em class="gene-name">

1844TP53</em>

1845 germline mutations in patients with osteosarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

18464</a>

1847,<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

18487</a>

1849,<a href="#cit/section_2.8">

18508</a>

1851]</p>

1852</section>

1853<section id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1925">

1854<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1925_toc">

1855<em class="gene-name">

1856RECQL4</em>

1857 mutations</h5>

1858<p id="_sm_CDR0000777834_1921" tabindex="-1">

1859Investigators analyzed whole-exome sequencing from the germline of 4,435 pediatric cancer patients at the St. Jude Children&#8217;s Research Hospital and 1,127 patients from the National Cancer Institute's Therapeutically Applicable Research to Generate Effective Treatment (TARGET) database. They identified 24 patients (0.43%) who harbored loss-of-function <em class="gene-name">

1860RECQL4</em>

1861 variants, including 5 of 249 patients (2.0%) with osteosarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.9">

18629</a>

1863] These <em class="gene-name">

1864RECQL4</em>

1865 variants were significantly overrepresented in children with osteosarcoma, the cancer most frequently observed in patients with Rothmund-Thomson syndrome, compared with 134,187 noncancer controls in the Genome Aggregation Database (gnomAD v2.1; <em>

1866P</em>

1867 = .00087; odds ratio, 7.1; 95% confidence interval, 2.9&#8211;17). Nine of the 24 individuals (38%) possessed the same c.1573delT (p.Cys525Alafs) variant located in the highly conserved DNA helicase domain, suggesting that disruption of this domain is central to oncogenesis.</p>

1868</section>

1911<td>

1912Bloom syndrome

2159

2160 <section id="_8">

2161

2162 <h2>

2163Cellular Classification of Osteosarcoma and UPS of Bone</h2>

2164

2165 <div id="_section_8" class="pdq-sections">

2166<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2167<h6>

2168In This Section</h6>

2169<ul>

2170<li>

2171<a href="#_48_toc">

2172Central (Medullary) Tumors</a>

2173</li>

2174<li>

2175<a href="#_49_toc">

2176Surface (Peripheral) Tumors</a>

2177</li>

2178<li>

2179<a href="#_1810_toc">

2180Extraosseous Osteosarcoma</a>

2181</li>

2182<li>

2183<a href="#_1936_toc">

2184Undifferentiated Pleomorphic Sarcoma (UPS) of Bone</a>

2185</li>

2186</ul>

2187</nav>

2188<p id="_9" tabindex="-1">

2189Osteosarcoma is a malignant tumor that is

2190characterized by the direct formation of bone or osteoid tissue by the tumor

2191cells. The World Health Organization&#8217;s histological classification [<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

21921</a>

2193] of bone

2194tumors separates the osteosarcomas into central (medullary) and surface

2195(peripheral) tumors [<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

21962</a>

2197,<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

21983</a>

2199] and recognizes a number of subtypes within each group.

2200</p>

2201<section id="_48">

2202<h3 id="_48_toc">

2203Central (Medullary) Tumors</h3>

2204<div class="pdq-content-list">

2205<ul id="_46">

2206<li>

2207 <strong>

2208Conventional central osteosarcomas.</strong>

2209 The most common pathological subtype is conventional central osteosarcoma, which

2210is characterized by areas of necrosis, atypical mitoses, and malignant

2211osteoid tissue and/or cartilage. The other subtypes are much less common, each occurring at a

2212frequency of less than 5%. </li>

2213<li>

2214 <strong>

2215Telangiectatic osteosarcomas.</strong>

2216[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

22174</a>

2218,<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

22195</a>

2220] Telangiectatic osteosarcoma may be confused

2221radiographically with an aneurysmal bone cyst or giant cell tumor. This

2222variant should be managed the same as a conventional osteosarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

22234</a>

2224,<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

22255</a>

2226]</li>

2227<li>

2228 <strong>

2229Intraosseous well-differentiated (low-grade) osteosarcomas.</strong>

2230</li>

2231<li>

2232<strong>

2233 Small-cell osteosarcomas.</strong>

2234</li>

2235</ul>

2236</div>

2237</section>

2238<section id="_49">

2239<h3 id="_49_toc">

2240Surface (Peripheral) Tumors</h3>

2241<p id="_460" tabindex="-1">

2242The terms <em>

2243parosteal</em>

2244 and <em>

2245periosteal</em>

2246 osteosarcoma are embedded in the literature and widely used. They are confusing to patients and practitioners. It would be more helpful to divide osteosarcoma by location and histological grade. High-grade osteosarcoma, sometimes referred to as conventional osteosarcoma, typically arises centrally and grows outward, destroying surrounding cortex and soft tissues, but there are unequivocal cases of high-grade osteosarcoma in surface locations.[<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

22476</a>

2248] Similarly, there are reports of low-grade osteosarcoma arising in the medullary cavity. </p>

2249<div class="pdq-content-list">

2250<ul id="_47">

2251<li>

2252 <strong>

2253Parosteal (juxtacortical) well-differentiated (low-grade) osteosarcomas.</strong>

2254[<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

22557</a>

2256,<a href="#cit/section_3.8">

22578</a>

2258] Parosteal osteosarcoma is defined as a lesion arising from the surface of the bone with a well-differentiated appearance on imaging and low-grade histological features.[<a href="#cit/section_3.9">

22599</a>

2260] The most common site for parosteal osteosarcoma is the posterior distal femur. Parosteal osteosarcoma occurs more often in older patients than does conventional high-grade osteosarcoma and is most common in patients aged 20 to 30 years. Parosteal osteosarcoma can be treated successfully with wide excision of the primary tumor alone.[<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

22617</a>

2262,<a href="#cit/section_3.10">

226310</a>

2264]</li>

2265<li>

2266 <strong>

2267Periosteal osteosarcomas (low-grade to intermediate-grade osteosarcomas).</strong>

2268[<a href="#cit/section_3.11">

226911</a>

2270-<a href="#cit/section_3.13">

227113</a>

2272] Periosteal osteosarcoma typically appears as a broad-based soft tissue mass with extrinsic erosion of the underlying bony cortex.[<a href="#cit/section_3.12">

227312</a>

2274] Pathology shows an intermediate grade of differentiation. Wide resection is essential. <p id="_1976" tabindex="-1">

2275A single-institution retrospective review identified 29 patients with periosteal osteosarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.11">

227611</a>

2277] The 5-year disease-free survival rate was 83%. The authors could not make a definitive statement regarding the benefits of adjuvant chemotherapy.</p>

2278<p id="_1977" tabindex="-1">

2279Another single-institution retrospective review identified 33 patients with periosteal osteosarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.13">

228013</a>

2281] The 10-year overall survival (OS) rate was 84%. The 10-year OS rate was 83% for patients who were treated with surgery alone and 86% for patients who were treated with surgery and chemotherapy. </p>

2282<p id="_1978" tabindex="-1">

2283The European Musculoskeletal Oncology Society retrospectively analyzed 119 patients with periosteal osteosarcoma; 17 patients had metastasis.[<a href="#cit/section_3.12">

228412</a>

2285] The OS rate was 89% at 5 years and 83% at 10 years. Eighty-one patients received chemotherapy; 50 of those patients received chemotherapy before definitive surgical resection. There was no difference in outcome between the patients who received chemotherapy and the patients who did not receive chemotherapy.</p>

2286</li>

2287<li>

2288<strong>

2289 High-grade surface osteosarcomas.</strong>

2290[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

22913</a>

2292,<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

22936</a>

2294,<a href="#cit/section_3.14">

229514</a>

2296]</li>

2297</ul>

2298</div>

2299</section>

2300<section id="_1810">

2301<h3 id="_1810_toc">

2302Extraosseous Osteosarcoma</h3>

2303<p id="_64" tabindex="-1">

2304Extraosseous osteosarcoma is a malignant mesenchymal neoplasm without direct attachment to the skeletal system. Previously, treatment for extraosseous osteosarcoma followed soft tissue sarcoma guidelines,[<a href="#cit/section_3.15">

230515</a>

2306] although a retrospective analysis of the cooperative German-Austrian-Swiss osteosarcoma study group identified a favorable outcome for extraosseous osteosarcoma treated with surgery and conventional osteosarcoma therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_3.16">

230716</a>

2308] </p>

2309</section>

2310<section id="_1936">

2311<h3 id="_1936_toc">

2312Undifferentiated Pleomorphic Sarcoma (UPS) of Bone</h3>

2313<p id="_1937" tabindex="-1">

2314UPS of bone should be distinguished from angiomatoid fibrous histiocytoma, a low-grade tumor that is usually noninvasive, small, and associated with an excellent outcome using surgery alone.[<a href="#cit/section_3.17">

231517</a>

2316] One study suggests similar event-free survival rates for UPS and osteosarcoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.18">

231718</a>

2318]

2319</p>

2320</section>

2321<h6 do-not-show="toc">

2322References</h6>

2323<ol>

2324<li id="section_3.1">

2325Schajowicz F, Sissons HA, Sobin LH: The World Health Organization's histologic classification of bone tumors. A commentary on the second edition. 2400

2401

2402

2403 <section id="_21">

2404

2405 <h2>

2406Staging and Site Information for Osteosarcoma and UPS of Bone</h2>

2407

2408 <div id="_section_21" class="pdq-sections">

2409<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2410<h6>

2411In This Section</h6>

2412<ul>

2413<li>

2414<a href="#_22_toc">

2415Localized Osteosarcoma </a>

2416</li>

2417<li>

2418<a href="#_24_toc">

2419Metastatic Osteosarcoma </a>

2420</li>

2421<li>

2422<a href="#_1813_toc">

2423Staging Evaluation</a>

2424</li>

2425</ul>

2426</nav>

2427<p id="_354" tabindex="-1">

2428Historically, the Enneking staging system for skeletal malignancies was used.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

24291</a>

2430] This system inferred the aggressiveness of the primary tumor by the descriptors <em>

2431intracompartmental</em>

2432 or <em>

2433extracompartmental</em>

2434. The American Joint Committee on Cancer's tumor-node-metastasis (TNM) staging system for malignant bone tumors is not widely used for pediatric osteosarcoma, and patients are not stratified on the basis of prognostic stage groups.</p>

2435<p id="_384" tabindex="-1">

2436For the purposes of treatment, osteosarcoma is described as one of the following: </p>

2437<div class="pdq-content-list">

2438<ul id="_1812">

2439<li>

2440<a href="/types/bone/hp/osteosarcoma-treatment-pdq#_22">

2441Localized</a>

2442. Patients without clinically detectable metastatic disease are considered to have localized osteosarcoma. </li>

2443<li>

2444<a href="/types/bone/hp/osteosarcoma-treatment-pdq#_24">

2445Metastatic</a>

2446. Patients with any site of metastasis at the time of initial presentation detected by routine clinical studies are considered to have metastatic osteosarcoma.</li>

2447</ul>

2448</div>

2449<section id="_22">

2450<h3 id="_22_toc">

2451Localized Osteosarcoma </h3>

2452<p id="_23" tabindex="-1">

2453Localized tumors are limited to the bone of origin. Patients with skip lesions confined to the bone that includes the primary tumor are considered to have localized disease if the skip lesions can be included in the planned surgical resection.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

24542</a>

2455] Approximately one-half of the tumors arise in the femur; of these, 80% are in the distal femur. Other primary sites, in

2456descending order of frequency, are the proximal tibia, proximal humerus, pelvis, jaw, fibula, and

2457ribs.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

24583</a>

2459]

2460Osteosarcoma of the head and neck is more likely to be low grade [<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

24614</a>

2462] and to arise in older patients than is osteosarcoma of the appendicular skeleton. </p>

2463</section>

2464<section id="_24">

2465<h3 id="_24_toc">

2466Metastatic Osteosarcoma </h3>

2467<p id="_25" tabindex="-1">

2468Radiologic evidence of metastatic tumor deposits is found in approximately 20% of patients at diagnosis, with 85% to 90% of

2469metastatic disease presenting in the lungs. The second most common site of metastasis

2470is another bone, which may be solitary or multiple.[<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

24715</a>

2472] </p>

2473<p id="_1948" tabindex="-1">

2474The syndrome of multifocal osteosarcoma refers to a presentation with multiple foci of osteosarcoma without a clear primary tumor, often with symmetrical metaphyseal involvement.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

24753</a>

2476]

2477</p>

2478</section>

2479<section id="_1813">

2480<h3 id="_1813_toc">

2481Staging Evaluation</h3>

2482<p id="_296" tabindex="-1">

2483For patients with confirmed osteosarcoma, in addition to plain radiographs of the primary site that include a single-plane view of the entire affected bone to assess for skip metastasis, pretreatment staging studies should include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

24846</a>

2485] </p>

2486<div class="pdq-content-list">

2487<ul id="_1814">

2488<li>

2489Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of the primary site to include the entire bone.</li>

2490<li>

2491Computed tomography (CT) scan, if MRI is not available.</li>

2492<li>

2493Bone scan.</li>

2494<li>

2495Posteroanterior and lateral chest radiograph.</li>

2496<li>

2497CT scan of the chest.</li>

2498</ul>

2499</div>

2500<p id="_1815" tabindex="-1">

2501Positron emission tomography (PET) using fluorine F 18-fludeoxyglucose is an optional staging modality.</p>

2502<p id="_1816" tabindex="-1">

2503A retrospective review of 206 patients with osteosarcoma compared bone scan, PET scan, and PET-CT scan for the detection of bone metastases.[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

25047</a>

2505] PET-CT was more sensitive and accurate than bone scan, and the combined use of both imaging studies achieved the highest sensitivity for diagnosing bone metastases in osteosarcoma.</p>

2506</section>

2544

2545 <section id="_26">

2546

2547 <h2>

2548Treatment Option Overview for Osteosarcoma and UPS of Bone</h2>

2549

2550 <div id="_section_26" class="pdq-sections">

2551<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2552<h6>

2553In This Section</h6>

2554<ul>

2555<li>

2556<a href="#_1949_toc">

2557Treatment Overview for Osteosarcoma and UPS of Bone</a>

2558</li>

2559</ul>

2560</nav>

2561<section id="_1949">

2562<h3 id="_1949_toc">

2563Treatment Overview for Osteosarcoma and UPS of Bone</h3>

2564<p id="_1819" tabindex="-1">

2565It is imperative that patients with proven or suspected

2566osteosarcoma have an initial evaluation by an orthopedic oncologist familiar

2567with the surgical management of this disease. This evaluation, which includes imaging studies, should be done

2568before the initial biopsy, because an inappropriately performed biopsy may

2569jeopardize a limb-sparing procedure. Additionally, protective weight bearing is recommended for patients with tumors of weight-bearing bones to prevent pathological fractures that could preclude limb-preserving surgery.</p>

2570<p id="_151" tabindex="-1">

2571Successful treatment generally requires the combination of effective systemic chemotherapy and complete resection of all clinically detectable disease.</p>

2572<p id="_27" tabindex="-1">

2573Randomized clinical trials have established that both neoadjuvant and adjuvant

2574chemotherapy are effective in preventing relapse in patients with clinically nonmetastatic tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

25751</a>

2576]; [<a href="#cit/section_5.2">

25772</a>

2578][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2579Level of evidence A1</a>

2580] The Pediatric Oncology Group (POG) conducted a study in which patients were randomly assigned to either immediate amputation or amputation after neoadjuvant therapy. A large percentage of patients declined to be assigned randomly, and the study was terminated without approaching the stated accrual goals. In the small number of patients treated, there was no difference in outcome between those who received preoperative chemotherapy and those who received postoperative chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

25813</a>

2582]</p>

2583<p id="_1820" tabindex="-1">

2584The treatment of osteosarcoma also depends on the histologic grade, as follows:</p>

2585<div class="pdq-content-list">

2586<ul id="_480">

2587<li>

2588<strong>

2589Low-grade osteosarcoma.</strong>

2590 Patients with low-grade osteosarcoma can be treated successfully by wide surgical resection alone, regardless of site of origin.</li>

2591<li>

2592<strong>

2593Intermediate-grade osteosarcoma.</strong>

2594 Pathologists sometimes characterize tumors as intermediate-grade osteosarcoma. It is difficult to make treatment decisions for patients with intermediate-grade tumors. When a tumor biopsy suggests an intermediate-grade osteosarcoma, an option is to proceed with wide resection. The availability of the entire tumor allows the pathologist to examine more tissue and evaluate soft tissue and lymphovascular invasion, which can often clarify the nature of the lesion.<p id="_1821" tabindex="-1">

2595If the lesion proves to have high-grade elements, systemic chemotherapy is indicated, just as it would be for any high-grade osteosarcoma. The POG performed a study in which patients with high-grade osteosarcoma were randomly assigned to either immediate definitive surgery followed by adjuvant chemotherapy or to an initial period of chemotherapy followed by definitive surgery.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

25963</a>

2597] The outcome was the same for both groups. Although the strategy of initial chemotherapy followed by definitive surgery has become an almost universally applied approach for osteosarcoma, this study suggests that there is no increased risk of treatment failure if definitive surgery is done before chemotherapy begins; this can help to clarify equivocal diagnoses of intermediate-grade osteosarcoma.</p>

2598</li>

2599<li>

2600<strong>

2601High-grade osteosarcoma.</strong>

2602 Patients with high-grade osteosarcoma require surgery and systemic chemotherapy whether the tumor arises in the conventional central location or on a bone surface.</li>

2603</ul>

2604</div>

2605<p id="_385" tabindex="-1">

2606Recognition of

2607intraosseous well-differentiated osteosarcoma and parosteal osteosarcoma is

2608important because patients with these tumor types have the most favorable prognosis and

2609can be treated successfully with wide excision of the primary tumor alone.[<a href="#cit/section_5.4">

26104</a>

2611,<a href="#cit/section_5.5">

26125</a>

2613] Patients with periosteal osteosarcoma have a generally good prognosis [<a href="#cit/section_5.6">

26146</a>

2615] and treatment is

2616guided by histologic grade.[<a href="#cit/section_5.5">

26175</a>

2618,<a href="#cit/section_5.7">

26197</a>

2620]</p>

2621<p id="_1817" tabindex="-1">

2622Patients with undifferentiated pleomorphic sarcoma (UPS) of bone are treated according to

2623osteosarcoma treatment protocols.[<a href="#cit/section_5.8">

26248</a>

2625] </p>

2626<p id="_353" tabindex="-1">

2627Imaging modalities such as dynamic magnetic resonance imaging or positron emission tomography scanning are under investigation as noninvasive methods to assess response.[<a href="#cit/section_5.9">

26289</a>

2629-<a href="#cit/section_5.17">

263017</a>

2631]</p>

2632<p id="_1886" tabindex="-1">

2633<a href="/types/bone/hp/osteosarcoma-treatment-pdq#_1880">

2634Table 2</a>

2894 <section id="_532">

2895

2896 <h2>

2897Special Considerations for the Treatment of Children With Cancer</h2>

2898

2899 <div id="_section_532" class="pdq-sections">

2900<p id="_533" tabindex="-1">

2901Cancer in children and adolescents is rare, although the overall incidence has been slowly increasing since 1975.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

29021</a>

2903] Children and adolescents with

2904cancer should be referred to medical centers that have a multidisciplinary team

2905of cancer specialists with experience treating the cancers that occur during

2906childhood and adolescence. This multidisciplinary team approach incorporates the skills

2907of the following health care professionals and

2908others to ensure that children receive treatment, supportive care, and

2909rehabilitation that will achieve optimal survival and quality of life:</p>

2910<div class="pdq-content-list">

2911<ul id="_1881">

2912<li>

2913Primary care physicians.</li>

2914<li>

2915Orthopedic surgeon experienced in bone

2916tumors.</li>

2917<li>

2918Pathologists.</li>

2919<li>

2920Radiation oncologists.</li>

2921<li>

2922Pediatric oncologists.</li>

2923<li>

2924Rehabilitation specialists.</li>

2925<li>

2926Pediatric nurse specialists.</li>

2927<li>

2928Social workers.</li>

2929<li>

2930Child-life professionals.</li>

2931<li>

2932Psychologists.</li>

2933</ul>

2934</div>

2935<p id="_1882" tabindex="-1">

2936For specific information about supportive care for children and adolescents with cancer, see the summaries on <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/information-summaries/supportive-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/information-summaries/supportive-care">

2937Supportive and Palliative Care</a>

2938. </p>

2939<p id="_534" tabindex="-1">

2940 The American Academy of

2941Pediatrics has outlined guidelines for pediatric cancer centers and their role in the treatment of

2942pediatric patients with cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

29432</a>

2944] At these centers, clinical trials are

2945available for most types of cancer that occur in children and

2946adolescents, and the opportunity to participate is offered to

2947most patients and their families. Clinical trials for children and adolescents with

2948cancer are generally designed to compare potentially better therapy with

2949current standard therapy. Most of the progress

2950in identifying curative therapies for childhood cancers has been achieved

2951through clinical trials. Information about ongoing clinical trials is

2952available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

2953National Cancer Institute's website</a>

2954.</p>

2955<p id="_1799" tabindex="-1">

2956 Childhood and adolescent cancer survivors require close monitoring because side effects of cancer therapy may persist or develop months or years after treatment. For specific information about the incidence, type, and monitoring of late effects in childhood and adolescent cancer survivors, see <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/late-effects-hp-pdq">

2957Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer</a>

2958. </p>

2959<h6 do-not-show="toc">

2960References</h6>

2961<ol>

2962<li id="section_6.1">

2963Smith MA, Altekruse SF, Adamson PC, et al.: Declining childhood and adolescent cancer mortality. Cancer 120 (16): 2497-506, 2014.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=24853691&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=24853691&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2964[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2965</li>

2966<li id="section_6.2">

2967American Academy of Pediatrics: Standards for pediatric cancer centers. Pediatrics 134 (2): 410-4, 2014. <a href="https://pediatrics.aappublications.org/content/134/2/410" title="https://pediatrics.aappublications.org/content/134/2/410">

2968Also available online</a>

2969. Last accessed May 19, 2023.</li>

2970</ol>

2971</div>

2973 </section>
